    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e451d607a1344f538bd8a07a377f1b76">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd8a70196-41ab-4afe-9b75-8caac811d7b7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:liztao@upc.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>liztao@upc.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Heavy Oil Processing, College of Chemical Engineering China University of Petroleum (East China), Qingdao, 266580 China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d66350132" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Cycling stability is the biggest challenge for cathodes of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which is directly affected by the structure design. Herein, the combination of high-entropy (HE) and multiphase structure is demonstrated to be helpful for maintaining the structure and improving the cycling stability. In the Ni/Mn/Cu/Ti/Sn five-component HE multiphase cathode, the multiple elements at transition metal sites can enlarge the lattice and stabilize the structure simultaneously without causing an obvious capacity drop, achieving the synergistic effect of multi-cations. In the HE cathodes consisting of P2 and O3 phases, the harmful phase transition in high-voltage is suppressed and the cycling performance is improved. A capacity retention of 77.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles is delivered, and an improved rate performance of 88.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 750&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is observed, better than that of the low-entropy multiphase cathode(P2 and O3) and the HE oxide single O3-phase cathode. The weighted average ionic radius(<i>WAIR</i>) of all transition metals is demonstrated critical for the formation of the phase composition in HE composites. Through comparing a series of HE and multiphase cathodes, an empirical range of <i>WAIR</i> is obtained, which shows guidance for the design of other cathode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400127-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400127-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid popularization of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) based electric vehicles, the shortage of lithium resources is attracting more and more attention,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0001_R_d66350119e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Energy Lett.">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0002_R_d66350119e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nature">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is even more serious when the LIBs are gradually applied in large-scale energy storage systems. Due to the advantages of high abundance of Na<sup>+</sup>, over 300 times higher than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possess a superior prospect in the application areas without strict requirements on energy density, such as large-scale energy storage and low-speed electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0003_R_d66350119e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Acc. Mater. Res. Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0004_R_d66350119e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In current SIBs, the cathode is of critical importance, since it directly determines the energy density and is also the most challenging component for the cycling life of cells. Among different kinds of cathode materials of SIBs, the layered oxide materials of Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = transition metal) show superiority in energy density,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0005_R_d66350119e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can be generally classified into P-type and O-type structures according to the different coordination environments of Na<sup>+</sup>, where P represents trigonal prismatic sites and O represents octahedral sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0006_R_d66350119e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Phys. B+C Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both P2 and O3 phases are typical layered oxides and possess their own advantages and challenges. In P2 phase, the trigonal prismatic sites allow direct diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> to adjacent sites, which is conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> migration because of its lower energy barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0007_R_d66350119e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources">7</a>-<a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0009_R_d66350119e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, lower Na content (&lt;0.8) in P2 phase limits the capacity and the phase transition of P2↔O2 in high voltage region causes large volume changes, deteriorating the cycling life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0010_R_d66350119e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochim. Acta Chem. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0011_R_d66350119e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the O3 phase owns higher Na content with enhanced capacity, but the octahedral site in O3 is unfavorable for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and limits the electrochemical kinetics. In addition, the complicated phase transitions of O3→O3′→P3→P3′→P3′′ suffer from poor structural reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0012_R_d66350119e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To stabilize the phase structure during extraction-intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, cationic doping is believed to be an effective method, which includes active elements doping (Cu,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0013_R_d66350119e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Ceram. Int. Adv. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0014_R_d66350119e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Eng. J.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0015_R_d66350119e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0017_R_d66350119e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Co,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0018_R_d66350119e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.) and inactive elements doping (Mg,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0019_R_d66350119e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0021_R_d66350119e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0022_R_d66350119e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Energy Chem.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sn,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0010_R_d66350119e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochim. Acta Chem. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0023_R_d66350119e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Energy Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0024_R_d66350119e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.). For example, Fe<sup>3+</sup> doping is beneficial in suppressing the irregular phase transition of P2 to O2 and provides extra capacity through the valence change of Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup>. However, the irreversible migration of Fe from TM layer to Na layer goes against the cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0025_R_d66350119e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti<sup>4+</sup> doping in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is helpful to mitigate the ordered arrangement of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy, at the expense of capacity loss, which is a common shortcoming of inactive elements doping in various cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0026_R_d66350119e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0027_R_d66350119e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Power Sources Sci. Adv. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to improve cycling stability without hurting the capacity, Hu et&nbsp;al. recently proposed a strategy of high-entropy(HE), using inactive metals to stabilize the phase structure and active metals to compensate the capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0028_R_d66350119e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0029_R_d66350119e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nature Joule">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O3-type NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> delivered a reversible capacity of 130.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;C and showed good cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0029_R_d66350119e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nature Joule">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are two different ways to describe the concept of HE. Qualitatively, materials consisting of five elements or more at the same crystal site are defined as HE materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0029_R_d66350119e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nature Joule">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Quantitatively, the values of configurational entropy (Δ<i>S</i><sub>c<i>onfig</i></sub>) greater than 1.5R (R is the universal gas constant) are considered as HE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0029_R_d66350119e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nature Joule">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To stabilize the structure and improve the cycling life, construction of multiphase structures is also an effective method, since they combine the advantages of various phase structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0030_R_d66350119e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Sci. Eng.">30</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0031_R_d66350119e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, the dual-phase structures of P and O are beneficial to enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and increase the capacity simultaneously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0032_R_d66350119e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Alloy. Compound.">32</a>-<a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0034_R_d66350119e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the P-type-based multiphase cathodes with P2 and P3 phases show improved structural stability and C-rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0035_R_d66350119e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0036_R_d66350119e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Electrochim. Acta">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The composite of P2/P3 and spinel structures combines the wide Na<sup>+</sup> channel of the P-type structure and the conductivity of the spinel structure, improving the rate performance of electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0037_R_d66350119e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nanoscale">37</a>-<a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0039_R_d66350119e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To understand the mechanism of composite structure on improved electrochemical stability, the “structural constraint effect” had also been proposed,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400127-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400127-bib-0040_R_d66350119e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with which, the irreversible phase change and the interlayer gliding were suppressed efficiently, improving the electrochemical performance. However, most of the multiphase structures were obtained unforeseeably at the early stage of preparation, owing to the lack of an effective guiding model or empirical rule. Since the TM components are key factors affecting the phase structure, finding a relationship between elemental components and phase structure will effectively improve the efficiency of multiphase structural design. In addition, the combination of the above two strategies of HE and multiphase structure has not been fully evaluated yet, which is possibly helpful for improving stability through a rational structural design. Therefore, it is desired to explore the combined functions of multiphase structure andHE on electrochemical performance, benefiting the future design of high-performance cathode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a series of HE multiphase materials were synthesized, containing five metals of Ni, Mn, Cu, Ti, and Sn in TM sites. From the comparison of these structures, it is found that the weighted average ionic radius(<i>WAIR</i>) of all TMs is critical for the composition of different phase structures. A larger <i>WAIR</i> facilitates the formation of O3 structure, while a smaller <i>WAIR</i> causes the generation of P2 phase. The multiphase structures (P2 and O3) of HE materials can be obtained in a specific <i>WAIR</i> range by alternating the metal contents in TM sites. For instance, when <i>WAIR</i> is between 0.583 and 0.637&nbsp;Å, P2 and O3 multiphase structures can be formed. To evaluate the effect of HE and multiphase in SIB cathodes, low-entropy multiphase structure of P2/O3-Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LE-P2/O3), HE multiphase P2/O3-Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.13</sub>Sn<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (HE-P2/O3) and HE single phase O3-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.25</sub>Cu<sub>0.13</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Sn<sub>0.22</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (HE-O3) were compared. It is observed that the cycling stability of the HE multiphase cathodes is better than that of the low-entropy multiphase (P2/O3) structure and HE single O3 structure. This improved electrochemical performance of HE multiphase structure can be attributed to the irregularly dispersed inactive TMs and the interlaced distribution of different phase structures, which stabilize the layered structure, suppress the interlayer gliding, and relieve the internal stress. The combination of HE and multiphase (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#aenm202400127-fig-0009">1</a>)&nbsp;gives a new pathway for the future design of long-life cathodes of SIBs.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400127-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4253b3fb-8a95-4ac3-8e93-9bc2611a88be/aenm202400127-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4253b3fb-8a95-4ac3-8e93-9bc2611a88be/aenm202400127-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e27852e-a53d-4824-9c98-1a03b5ae106f/aenm202400127-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4253b3fb-8a95-4ac3-8e93-9bc2611a88be/aenm202400127-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
